So v=(5/4, -3√15/4). |v| = sqrt((5/4)^2 + (3√15/4)^2)= sqrt(25/16 + 9*15/16)= sqrt(25+135)/4 = sqrt(160)/4 = (4√10)/4 = √10. So |v|=√10. Good.

Now t_E = -2(A·v)/|v|^2. A=(1/4, √15/4). v=(5/4, -3√15/4). A·v = (1/4)*(5/4) + (√15/4)*(-3√15/4)= (5/16) - (3*15/16)= (5-45)/16 = -40/16 = -5/2. |v|^2=10. So t_E = -2*(-5/2)/10 = (5)/10 = 1/2. So E = A + (1/2)v = (1/4 + (1/2)*(5/4), √15/4 + (1/2)*(-3√15/4)) = (1/4 + 5/8, √15/4 - 3√15/8) = (2/8+5/8=7/8, (2√15/8-3√15/8)=-√15/8). So E=(7/8, -√15/8). t_F = -2((A-N)·v)/|v|^2. N=(2,0). A-N = (1/4-2, √15/4-0) = (-7/4, √15/4). Dot with v: (-7/4)*(5/4) + (√15/4)*(-3√15/4)= -35/16 - 3*15/16 = -35/16 -45/16 = -80/16 = -5. So t_F = -2*(-5)/10 = 10/10=1. So F = A + 1*v = A+v = (1/4+5/4, √15/4 -3√15/4) = (6/4=3/2, -2√15/4 = -√15/2). So F = P. So F = P. Good.

Now B = (1/4, -√15/4). So points: B(0.25, -0.9682458366), E(0.875, -0.4841229183), F(1.5, -1.936491673). Now compute circumcenter O of triangle BEF.

We can compute using perpendicular bisectors. Let's do exact symbolic.

Let B = (1/4, -√15/4). E = (7/8, -√15/8). F = (3/2, -√15/2). Note that all y-coordinates are multiples of √15. Let's denote s = √15. Then:

B = (1/4, -s/4)
E = (7/8, -s/8)
F = (3/2, -s/2)

We can find the circumcenter by solving for O = (x0, y0) such that distances squared to B, E, F equal.

Let O = (x, y). Then:

(1) (x - 1/4)^2 + (y + s/4)^2 = (x - 7/8)^2 + (y + s/8)^2
(2) (x - 1/4)^2 + (y + s/4)^2 = (x - 3/2)^2 + (y + s/2)^2

Expand differences.

From (1): (x^2 - (1/2)x + 1/16) + (y^2 + (s/2)y + s^2/16) = (x^2 - (7/4)x + 49/64) + (y^2 + (s/4)y + s^2/64). Cancel x^2,y^2. Then:

- (1/2)x + 1/16 + (s/2)y + s^2/16 = - (7/4)x + 49/64 + (s/4)y + s^2/64.

Bring all terms: (-1/2)x + (7/4)x + (s/2)y - (s/4)y + 1/16 - 49/64 + s^2/16 - s^2/64 = 0.

Compute coefficients: x: (-2/4 + 7/4) = 5/4 x. y: (2s/4 - s/4) = s/4 y. Constant: 1/16 - 49/64 = 4/64 - 49/64 = -45/64. s^2/16 - s^2/64 = 4s^2/64 - s^2/64 = 3s^2/64. So constant = (-45 + 3s^2)/64.

But s^2 = 15, so 3*15=45. So constant = (-45+45)/64 = 0. So equation reduces to: (5/4)x + (s/4)y = 0 => 5x + s y = 0 => s y = -5x => y = -5x/s.

So (1) gives: y = -5x/√15. That is a line through origin? Indeed O lies on line through origin with slope -5/√15. But note that x0? Actually O is on that line.

Now from (2): (x - 1/4)^2 + (y + s/4)^2 = (x - 3/2)^2 + (y + s/2)^2. Expand similarly:

Left: (x^2 - (1/2)x + 1/16) + (y^2 + (s/2)y + s^2/16). Right: (x^2 - 3x + 9/4) + (y^2 + s y + s^2/4). Cancel x^2,y^2. Then:

- (1/2)x + 1/16 + (s/2)y + s^2/16 = -3x + 9/4 + s y + s^2/4.

Bring terms: (-1/2)x + 3x + (s/2)y - s y + 1/16 - 9/4 + s^2/16 - s^2/4 = 0.

Compute x: (-0.5+3)=2.5 = 5/2 x. y: (s/2 - s)= -s/2 y. Constant: 1/16 - 9/4 = 1/16 - 36/16 = -35/16. s^2/16 - s^2/4 = s^2/16 - 4s^2/16 = -3s^2/16 = -3*15/16 = -45/16. So constant = -35/16 -45/16 = -80/16 = -5. So equation: (5/2)x - (s/2)y - 5 = 0 => Multiply 2: 5x - s y - 10 = 0 => s y = 5x - 10 => y = (5x - 10)/s.

Now we have two equations for O: y = -5x/s from (1), and y = (5x - 10)/s from (2). Equate: -5x/s = (5x - 10)/s => -5x = 5x - 10 => 10x = 10 => x = 1. Then y = -5*1/s = -5/√15 = -√15/3? Actually -5/√15 = -5√15/15 = -√15/3 ≈ -1.290994. So O = (1, -√15/3). Radius squared: distance to B: (1-1/4)^2 + (-√15/3 + √15/4)^2 = (3/4)^2 + ( (-4√15/12 + 3√15/12)=(-√15/12) )^2 = (9/16) + (15/144) = (9/16) + (5/48) = (27/48 + 5/48)=32/48=2/3. So radius = √(2/3) ≈ 0.81649658. Good.

Now H = (p_x, h_y) = (3/2, - d y_A/(d+R+r) )? Actually we computed H from formula: H = (d(d+R-x_A)/(d+R+r), - d y_A/(d+R+r)). With d=2, R=2, x_A=1/4, y_A=√15/4. Then d+R-x_A = 2+2-0.25=3.75=15/4. d+R+r = 2+2+1=5. So H_x = 2*(15/4)/5 = (30/4)/5= (15/2)/5=15/10=3/2=1.5. H_y = -2*(√15/4)/5 = - (√15/2)/5 = -√15/10. So H = (3/2, -√15/10). v = (5/4, -3√15/4). So line through H parallel to v: param H + u v. We want distance from O to this line. Compute vector from H to O: O - H = (1 - 3/2, -√15/3 + √15/10) = (-1/2, (-10√15/30 + 3√15/30)= -7√15/30). So H->O = (-1/2, -7√15/30). Now cross product with v: det( H->O, v ) = (-1/2)*( -3√15/4 ) - (-7√15/30)*(5/4) = ( (1/2)*(3√15/4) ) + ( (7√15/30)*(5/4) ) = (3√15/8) + (35√15/120) = (3√15/8) + (7√15/24) = common denominator 24: (9√15/24 + 7√15/24)=16√15/24=2√15/3. So |det| = 2√15/3. |v| = √10. So distance = (2√15/3)/√10 = (2√15)/(3√10)= (2√(15/10))/3 = (2√(3/2))/3 = (2√(3)/√2)/3 = (2√3)/(3√2)= (2√6)/(6)= √6/3? Actually (2√3)/(3√2)= (2√6)/(6)= √6/3. So distance = √6/3 ≈ 0.81649658. That equals radius! Indeed radius = √(2/3) = √2/√3 = √6/3. Yes, √6/3 ≈ 0.81649658. So distance equals radius, so line is tangent. Great.

Thus in this numeric example, the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to (BEF). So the claim holds.

Now we need to prove it in general. We can attempt to compute O (circumcenter of BEF) and distance from O to line through H parallel to v, and show it equals radius.

Given that the configuration is symmetric about the x-axis? Actually, we have B as reflection of A across x-axis, and E and F lie on line AP. So maybe we can find that the circumcircle of BEF is the image of the circle through A, E, F? Not exactly.

Alternatively, we can use vector algebra and derive that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent if and only if some relation holds, which we can verify using coordinates.

Let's attempt to compute O in terms of A, v, t_E, t_F, B.

We have points: B = (x_A, -y_A). Since A = (x_A, y_A). Let’s denote A = (a_x, a_y) with a_y>0. Then B = (a_x, -a_y). v = (v_x, v_y) direction of AP. We have t_E and t_F given by:

t_E = -2(A·v)/|v|^2.
t_F = -2((A-N)·v)/|v|^2.

But note that A-N = (a_x-d, a_y). And we have v_x = (R^2 - r^2)/(2d). v_y = p_y - a_y =? Actually p_y = -p_x (a_x+r)/a_y. So v_y = -p_x (a_x+r)/a_y - a_y = -(p_x(a_x+r)+a_y^2)/a_y = -(p_x(a_x+r)+r^2 - a_x^2)/a_y. But maybe we can find a simpler expression for v_y in terms of d,r,R and a_x,a_y. Since a_x = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d). Then we can compute v_y as a function.

We can also compute t_E and t_F in terms of the distances? Alternatively, we can use the fact that E is the second intersection of line AP with Ω. Since Ω is circle centered M at (0,0) radius r, and A is on Ω, we have that AE is a chord of Ω. The midpoint of AE is the projection of M onto line AP. Similarly, for F on Γ, midpoint of AF is projection of N onto line AP. So we can find t_E and t_F using the projection.

Let’s denote the foot of perpendicular from M to line AP. Since M is at origin, the foot is the projection of 0 onto line AP. That foot is some point on line AP. The chord AE is such that the line from M to midpoint of AE is perpendicular to AE. So the midpoint of AE is the projection of M onto line AP. So if we let param on line AP: A + t v, then the projection of M onto line AP is given by t0 = -A·v/|v|^2? Actually, the projection of point X onto line through A with direction v is given by: X_proj = A + ((X - A)·v)/|v|^2 v. For X=M=(0,0), then (M-A)·v = -A·v. So projection = A - (A·v)/|v|^2 v. That is the foot. The midpoint of AE is that foot. Since A and E are symmetric with respect to this foot (the foot is midpoint of chord AE because M is center). So if foot = A + t_mid v, then t_mid = -A·v/|v|^2. And E = A + t_E v, where t_E = 2 t_mid = -2A·v/|v|^2. That matches our t_E. Good.

Similarly, for Γ: center N, foot of perpendicular from N to line AP is midpoint of AF. So t_mid_N = ((N-A)·v)/|v|^2? Actually, projection of N onto line: N_proj = A + ((N - A)·v)/|v|^2 v. Then midpoint of AF = N_proj, and F = A + 2 t_mid_N v = A + 2((N-A)·v)/|v|^2 v. So t_F = 2((N-A)·v)/|v|^2. But earlier we had t_F = -2((A-N)·v)/|v|^2 = 2((N-A)·v)/|v|^2. Yes, consistent.

So we have t_E = -2 A·v/|v|^2, t_F = 2 (N-A)·v/|v|^2.

Now note that A, N, M are known. v = P-A, but we may not need P explicitly. We can find v in terms of coordinates. Since P is circumcenter of ACD, we can find v using the fact that P lies on perpendicular bisectors. But maybe we can find a simpler relation: v is perpendicular to something? Because MP is perpendicular to AC. And we know M is origin, P is some point. v = P-A. But AC is chord of Ω from A to C. Since M is center, the line from M to midpoint of AC is perpendicular to AC. So AC is perpendicular to line from M to midpoint of AC. But we also have MP is perpendicular to AC. So MP is parallel to line from M to midpoint of AC? Actually, both MP and the line from M to midpoint of AC are perpendicular to AC, so they are parallel. But they both pass through M? No, MP passes through M and P; the line from M to midpoint of AC also passes through M. So they are the same line! Because there is a unique line through M perpendicular to AC. So MP is exactly the line through M perpendicular to AC, and it also contains the midpoint of AC. So P lies on the line through M that is perpendicular to AC. But we also have that P is the circumcenter of ACD, so it lies on perpendicular bisector of AC, which is the line through midpoint of AC perpendicular to AC. That line is exactly the line through M perpendicular to AC? Wait, the perpendicular bisector of AC is the line perpendicular to AC passing through the midpoint of AC. M lies on that line because MA=MC, so M is equidistant from A and C, so M lies on the perpendicular bisector of AC. Thus the perpendicular bisector of AC is the line through M and the midpoint of AC, which is the same as the line through M perpendicular to AC. So indeed MP is that line. So P lies on the line through M perpendicular to AC. That's consistent.

Thus v = P-A is some vector. We might find that v is proportional to (R^2 - r^2, something). But maybe we can avoid explicit coordinates by using properties of orthocenters and radical axes.

Let me think synthetically.

Given circles Ω (center M, radius r) and Γ (center N, radius R), intersecting at A and B. Line MN meets Ω at C (on opposite side of M from N) and Γ at D (on opposite side of N from M). Another approach: Use inversion with center A that sends circles Ω and Γ to lines. Let's try that.

Perform inversion with center A and arbitrary radius (say 1). Then:

- Ω becomes a line ℓ_Ω (since Ω passes through A). Similarly, Γ becomes a line ℓ_Γ.
- Points C and D: C is on Ω, so its image C' lies on ℓ_Ω. Since C is the intersection of Ω with MN, but after inversion, MN becomes a circle through A (since MN does not pass through A). That might be messy.
- The line AP is through A, so it maps to itself (as a line through A). So points E and F on AP map to E' and F' on the same line. Since E is second intersection of AP with Ω, and Ω maps to line ℓ_Ω, then E' is the intersection of AP with ℓ_Ω (since inversion sends the circle through A to line not through A, and the line AP through A maps to itself, so the second intersection becomes the intersection of AP with the image line). So E' = AP ∩ ℓ_Ω. Similarly, F' = AP ∩ ℓ_Γ.
- Point B is on both circles, so B' lies on both lines ℓ_Ω and ℓ_Γ. So B' = ℓ_Ω ∩ ℓ_Γ. So B' is the intersection of the two lines (images of circles).
- Triangle BEF: points B, E, F. Under inversion, the circumcircle of BEF maps to the circumcircle of B' E' F'? Actually, inversion preserves circles (though may map circles not through A to circles). Since B, E, F are not necessarily on a circle through A? But the circle (BEF) may or may not pass through A. Inversion will map it to a circle (or line) through B', E', F'. But H is orthocenter of triangle PMN; we would need to invert H as well. That might become complicated.

Given the success of coordinate approach in the example, we can attempt a general analytic proof. Let's set up coordinates as before: M=(0,0), N=(d,0) with d>0, r<R, and d satisfies |R-r| < d < R+r. We have:

A = (x_A, y_A) with y_A>0, x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d), y_A = √(r^2 - x_A^2).
B = (x_A, -y_A).

C = (-r,0), D = (d+R,0).

P = circumcenter of ACD. Since C and D on x-axis, P lies on vertical line x = ( -r + d+R )/2 = p_x. So p_x = (d+R-r)/2.

Now compute p_y from PA=PC. We have PA^2 = (p_x - x_A)^2 + (p_y - y_A)^2, PC^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2. Set equal:

(p_x - x_A)^2 + (p_y - y_A)^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2.

=> p_x^2 -2p_x x_A + x_A^2 + p_y^2 -2p_y y_A + y_A^2 = p_x^2 + 2r p_x + r^2 + p_y^2.

Cancel p_x^2, p_y^2: -2p_x x_A + x_A^2 -2p_y y_A + y_A^2 = 2r p_x + r^2.

But x_A^2+y_A^2 = r^2, so:

-2p_x x_A -2p_y y_A + r^2 = 2r p_x + r^2 => -2p_x x_A -2p_y y_A = 2r p_x => p_x x_A + p_y y_A = -r p_x => p_y y_A = -p_x (x_A + r) => p_y = -p_x (x_A+r)/y_A.

Thus P = (p_x, p_y) with p_y negative.

Now v = P - A = (p_x - x_A, p_y - y_A). We have p_x - x_A = (d+R-r)/2 - (d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d) = (1/(2d))[ d(d+R-r) - (d^2+r^2-R^2) ] = (1/(2d))[ d^2+dr -dr - d^2 - r^2 + R^2 ] = (R^2 - r^2)/(2d). So v_x = (R^2 - r^2)/(2d).

Now v_y = p_y - y_A = -p_x (x_A+r)/y_A - y_A = -(p_x(x_A+r) + y_A^2)/y_A = -(p_x(x_A+r) + r^2 - x_A^2)/y_A.

We can compute p_x(x_A+r). Since x_A = (d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d). Then x_A+r = (d^2+r^2-R^2+2dr)/(2d) = ((d+r)^2 - R^2)/(2d) = (d+r-R)(d+r+R)/(2d). So:

p_x(x_A+r) = ((d+R-r)/2) * ((d+r-R)(d+r+R)/(2d)) = (d+R-r)(d+r-R)(d+r+R)/(4d).

Thus v_y = -[ (d+R-r)(d+r-R)(d+r+R)/(4d) + r^2 - x_A^2 ]/y_A.

Now r^2 - x_A^2 = y_A^2. So:

v_y = -[ (d+R-r)(d+r-R)(d+r+R)/(4d) + y_A^2 ]/y_A = -[ (d+R-r)(d+r-R)(d+r+R)/(4d) + y_A^2 ]/y_A.

But we might express y_A^2 in terms of d,r,R. From earlier:

y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2 = r^2 - ((d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d))^2 = (4d^2 r^2 - (d^2+r^2-R^2)^2)/(4d^2) = ( (2dr)^2 - (d^2+r^2-R^2)^2 )/(4d^2) = ( (2dr - (d^2+r^2-R^2))(2dr + (d^2+r^2-R^2)) )/(4d^2).

Now 2dr - (d^2+r^2-R^2) = R^2 - (d^2 -2dr + r^2) = R^2 - (d-r)^2 = (R - (d-r))(R + (d-r)). And 2dr + (d^2+r^2-R^2) = (d+r)^2 - R^2 = (d+r-R)(d+r+R). So:

y_A^2 = [ (R-d+r)(R+d-r)(d+r-R)(d+r+R) ]/(4d^2). Note that (R-d+r) = (R+r-d). So we have:

y_A^2 = (R+r-d)(R+d-r)(d+r-R)(d+r+R)/(4d^2). Good.

Now compute (d+R-r)(d+r-R)(d+r+R)/(4d) + y_A^2. Factor (d+r+R) common? Actually:

(d+R-r)(d+r-R)(d+r+R)/(4d) = (d+r+R)*(d+R-r)*(d+r-R)/(4d). And y_A^2 = (d+r+R)*(R+r-d)*(R+d-r)/(4d^2). So sum = (d+r+R)/(4d) * [ (d+R-r)(d+r-R) + (R+r-d)(R+d-r)/d ]? Because y_A^2 has denominator 4d^2, so factor (d+r+R)/(4d) times? Let's write:

y_A^2 = (d+r+R)(R+r-d)(R+d-r)/(4d^2). So sum S = (d+R-r)(d+r-R)(d+r+R)/(4d) + (d+r+R)(R+r-d)(R+d-r)/(4d^2) = (d+r+R)/(4d) * [ (d+R-r)(d+r-R) + (R+r-d)(R+d-r)/d ].

Thus v_y = -S / y_A = - (d+r+R)/(4d y_A) * [ (d+R-r)(d+r-R) + (R+r-d)(R+d-r)/d ].

But maybe we can find a simpler expression for v_y in terms of v_x and something. Note that v_x = (R^2 - r^2)/(2d). And we have that A, P, E, F are collinear. Also, we can compute t_E and t_F.

We need O, circumcenter of BEF. B=(x_A, -y_A). E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v. So B, E, F are three points. Since B is symmetric of A across x-axis, and v is some vector, maybe we can find O as intersection of perpendicular bisectors. We can attempt to find O in terms of A, v, t_E, t_F, and B. There might be a known formula: For points on a line, the circumcenter of triangle formed by a point and two points on a line can be expressed via the reflection of that point across the line? Actually, consider a line l and a point B not on l. Let two points E, F on l. Then the circumcenter O of triangle BEF is the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of BF and the perpendicular bisector of BE. This O lies on the perpendicular bisector of the segment joining B to the midpoint of EF? Not exactly.

Alternatively, we can compute O directly by solving linear equations: O satisfies that O is equidistant from B and E, and from B and F. So (O - B)^2 = (O - E)^2 and (O - B)^2 = (O - F)^2. These give linear equations because squares cancel. Indeed, for two points X,Y, the set of points O with OX=OY is the perpendicular bisector line, which is linear: 2O·(Y-X) = |Y|^2 - |X|^2. So we can write:

2O·(E - B) = |E|^2 - |B|^2.
2O·(F - B) = |F|^2 - |B|^2.

Thus O is the solution of a linear system. Since E and F are on line AP, and B is symmetric, we might find that O lies on some line. Then we need to compute distance from O to line through H parallel to v. That distance is |(O - H) × v|/|v|. And we want that to equal radius R_{BEF} = distance from O to B. So condition is: |(O - H) × v|/|v| = |O - B|. Equivalently, |(O - H) × v|^2 = |v|^2 |O - B|^2.

This is an equation that we can try to verify using coordinates. Since we have symbolic expressions, we can attempt to compute O in terms of A, v, t_E, t_F, B, and then compute the cross product magnitude squared and compare with |v|^2 |O-B|^2. This will involve many algebra but we can simplify using known relations: A, B are symmetric; v_x and v_y; t_E and t_F given by dot products.

2X·(F-B) = |F|^2 - |B|^2.   (II)

Let’s denote D1 = E-B, D2 = F-B.

Now, note that we can write E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v. And B = (a_x, -a_y). So D1 = (t_E v1, a_y + t_E v2 + a_y?) Wait, B = (a_x, -a_y). So E-B = (a_x + t_E v1 - a_x, a_y + t_E v2 + a_y) = (t_E v1, 2a_y + t_E v2). Similarly, D2 = (t_F v1, 2a_y + t_F v2).

Now, compute |E|^2 - |B|^2 = (|A|^2 + 2t_E A·v + t_E^2|v|^2) - |A|^2 = 2t_E A·v + t_E^2|v|^2. But as we saw, t_E = -2(A·v)/|v|^2, so 2t_E A·v = -4(A·v)^2/|v|^2, and t_E^2|v|^2 = 4(A·v)^2/|v|^2, sum = 0. So |E|^2 - |B|^2 = 0. Similarly, |F|^2 - |B|^2 = 2t_F A·v + t_F^2|v|^2. So (II) gives: 2X·D2 = |F|^2 - |B|^2.

Now, we need to solve for X. Write the linear system:

2X·D1 = 0.   (I')
2X·D2 = |F|^2 - |B|^2.   (II')

Since D1 is not zero (unless v1=0 and 2a_y+t_E v2=0, but v1 = (R^2-r^2)/(2d) which is zero only if R=r, but r<R, so v1>0, so D1 has nonzero first component). So we can solve.

From (I'): X·D1 = 0. So X is perpendicular to D1. That is a linear condition.

We can find X explicitly. Let’s set up coordinates. Write D1 = (d1_x, d1_y) = (t_E v1, 2a_y + t_E v2). Then (I') says: d1_x X_x + d1_y X_y = 0 => X_y = -(d1_x/d1_y) X_x if d1_y ≠ 0. But we can also solve using vector algebra.

Since we have two equations, we can solve for X as intersection of two lines. Let’s denote:

Equation (I'): 2 (t_E v1) X_x + 2 (2a_y + t_E v2) X_y = 0 => t_E v1 X_x + (2a_y + t_E v2) X_y = 0.   (1)
Equation (II'): 2 (t_F v1) X_x + 2 (2a_y + t_F v2) X_y = |F|^2 - |B|^2 => t_F v1 X_x + (2a_y + t_F v2) X_y = (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2.   (2)

We can solve this linear system for X_x, X_y. The solution will give O = circumcenter of BEF.

Now, we also have H coordinates: H = (h_x, h_y) = (d - k(r+x_A), -k a_y) where k = d/(d+R+r). But note: we can also express H in terms of A, M, N? We have H = (d - d(r+h)/ (d+R+r)? Actually, from earlier: H_x = d - d(r+h)/(d+R+r), H_y = -d a/(d+R+r). So H = (d - α (r+h), -α a) where α = d/(d+R+r). Since r+h =? We have h = x_A, a = y_A.

Now we need to show that the line through H with direction v (i.e., line l: H + λ v) is tangent to circle (BEF). That is equivalent to showing that the distance from O to l equals the radius OB. Since O is circumcenter, radius R = OB. So we need:

| (O - H) × v | / |v| = |O - B|.

Square both sides: |(O-H) × v|^2 = |v|^2 |O-B|^2.

Now note that |(O-H) × v|^2 = |O-H|^2 |v|^2 - ( (O-H)·v )^2. So condition becomes:

|O-H|^2 |v|^2 - ( (O-H)·v )^2 = |v|^2 |O-B|^2 => |O-H|^2 - ( (O-H)·v )^2/|v|^2 = |O-B|^2 => |O-H|^2 - ((O-H)·v̂)^2 = |O-B|^2, where v̂ = v/|v|.

This is a condition on the projection of O-H onto v. This is reminiscent of the fact that the line through H parallel to v is tangent if and only if H lies on the circle that is the reflection of something? Actually, we can interpret: For a given circle with center O and radius R, the set of points H such that the line through H parallel to v is tangent to the circle is the set of points H that are at distance R from O in the direction perpendicular to v? Not exactly: The condition that the line through H with direction v is tangent means that the foot of perpendicular from O to that line is at distance R. That foot is O - ((O-H)·v̂) v̂. So the distance from O to the line is |(O-H) × v̂|. And we require that to equal R. So |(O-H) × v̂| = R. That is equivalent to |O-H|^2 - ((O-H)·v̂)^2 = R^2. So indeed, the condition is: |O-H|^2 - ((O-H)·v̂)^2 = R^2. This is a quadratic equation in H.

Thus we need to verify that for H defined as orthocenter of triangle PMN, this holds with O and R from circle (BEF). So we need to compute O and R in terms of A, v, B, and then compute |O-H|^2 - ((O-H)·v̂)^2 and show it equals |O-B|^2. This seems algebraic.

Maybe we can find that O lies on the line through H perpendicular to v? Or that O-B is perpendicular to something.

Let’s try complex numbers.

Its imaginary part p_y =? We can compute using complex numbers: P is the circumcenter of points z1,z2,z3. But we can avoid that by using geometry: Since C and D are real, we can find P by intersecting the perpendicular bisector of CD (vertical line x = p_x) with the perpendicular bisector of AC. The perpendicular bisector of AC: line through midpoint of AC, perpendicular to AC. In complex numbers, the condition for a point Z to be on the perpendicular bisector of points A and C is: (Z - (A+C)/2) * (A-C) is purely imaginary? Actually, the perpendicular bisector consists of points Z such that |Z-A| = |Z-C|. So we need to solve |Z - A| = |Z - C|. This is linear in Z and \bar{Z}. Write Z = x + iy. Then (x - Re(A))^2 + (y - Im(A))^2 = (x + r)^2 + y^2. This gave earlier linear equation: -2x Re(A) + |A|^2 -2y Im(A) = 2r x + r^2? Let's do complex: |Z - A|^2 = |Z - C|^2 => (Z-A)(\bar{Z}-\bar{A}) = (Z-C)(\bar{Z}-\bar{C}) => |Z|^2 - Z\bar{A} - \bar{Z}A + |A|^2 = |Z|^2 - Z\bar{C} - \bar{Z}C + |C|^2 => -Z\bar{A} - \bar{Z}A + |A|^2 = -Z\bar{C} - \bar{Z}C + |C|^2 => Z(\bar{C}-\bar{A}) + \bar{Z}(C-A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2. Since C = -r is real, \bar{C}=C. So equation: Z(C - \bar{A}) + \bar{Z}(C - A) = |C|^2 - |A|^2. But note that |C|^2 = r^2, |A|^2 = r^2, so RHS = 0. Thus Z(C - \bar{A}) + \bar{Z}(C - A) = 0. Write Z = x+iy, A = h+ia. Then C = -r. So C - \bar{A} = -r - (h - ia) = -(r+h) + i a. And C - A = -r - (h+ia) = -(r+h) - i a. So equation: Z(-(r+h) + i a) + \bar{Z}(-(r+h) - i a) = 0 => - (r+h)(Z+\bar{Z}) + i a (Z - \bar{Z}) = 0 => - (r+h)(2x) + i a (2iy) = -2(r+h)x -2a y = 0 => (r+h)x + a y = 0. So indeed the perpendicular bisector of AC is line: (r+h)x + a y = 0. And we know that P lies on this line and also on x = p_x. So we can find p_y: (r+h)p_x + a p_y = 0 => p_y = - (r+h)p_x / a. So P = p_x + i p_y with p_y negative. Good.

Thus P = p_x - i (r+h)p_x / a = p_x (1 - i (r+h)/a). Since a>0, p_y negative.

Now, v = P - A = (p_x - h) + i (p_y - a). But we have p_x - h = (d+R-r)/2 - h. And we already computed p_x - h = (R^2 - r^2)/(2d). Let's verify using complex: h = Re(A) = r cosθ. And p_x = (d+R-r)/2. So p_x - h = (d+R-r)/2 - r cosθ. But also we have relation from circle: r^2 = h^2 + a^2, and (d-h)^2 + a^2 = R^2 => r^2 - h^2 = R^2 - (d-h)^2 => r^2 - h^2 = R^2 - d^2 + 2dh - h^2 => r^2 = R^2 - d^2 + 2dh => 2dh = r^2 - R^2 + d^2 => h = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d). So p_x - h = (d+R-r)/2 - (d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d) = (1/(2d))[ d(d+R-r) - (d^2+r^2-R^2) ] = (1/(2d))[ d^2+dr-dr - d^2 - r^2 + R^2 ] = (R^2 - r^2)/(2d). Good.

Now p_y - a = - (r+h)p_x / a - a = -( (r+h)p_x + a^2 )/a = -( (r+h)p_x + (r^2 - h^2) )/a. So v = (R^2 - r^2)/(2d) - i [ (r+h)p_x + (r^2 - h^2) ]/a.

Now t_E = -2 (A·v)/|v|^2, but in complex numbers, the dot product is Re(A \bar{v}). So A·v = Re(A \bar{v}). And |v|^2 = v \bar{v}. So t_E = -2 Re(A \bar{v}) / (v \bar{v}). And t_F = 2 Re((N-A) \bar{v}) / (v \bar{v}). Since N = d real, N-A = d - A.

Now we need O, circumcenter of B, E, F. B = \bar{A} (since A = h+ia, B = h-ia). So B = \bar{A}. E = A + t_E v. F = A + t_F v.

We can try to compute O using the formula for circumcenter in complex numbers. There is formula: For three points z1, z2, z3, the circumcenter is given by:

O = ( (z1|z2|^2(z3-z2) + z2|z3|^2(z1-z3) + z3|z1|^2(z2-z1) ) / ( z1(z3-z2) + z2(z1-z3) + z3(z2-z1) )? Actually, there is formula for circumcenter: O = ( (z1\bar{z1})(z2-z3) + (z2\bar{z2})(z3-z1) + (z3\bar{z3})(z1-z2) ) / ( \bar{z1}(z2-z3) + \bar{z2}(z3-z1) + \bar{z3}(z1-z2) ). But that's for the circumcenter of triangle? Let's derive: The circumcenter is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors; the condition |z-O| = |z1-O| = |z2-O| = |z3-O|. We can set up equations: |z-O|^2 = |z1-O|^2 => z\bar{z} - z\bar{O} - \bar{z}O + |O|^2 = |z1|^2 - z1\bar{O} - \bar{z1}O + |O|^2 => z\bar{z} - z\bar{O} - \bar{z}O = |z1|^2 - z1\bar{O} - \bar{z1}O. For three points, this gives linear equations in O and \bar{O}. We can solve.

Given the algebraic approach, we can try to compute O in terms of A, v, and then compute |O-H|^2 - ((O-H)·v̂)^2 = |O-B|^2. This is an identity we need to verify. Let's compute O explicitly using the linear equations we derived earlier. We have:

(1) t_E v1 X_x + (2a + t_E v2) X_y = 0.
(2) t_F v1 X_x + (2a + t_F v2) X_y = (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2.

We can solve for X_x, X_y. But note that t_E = -2 (A·v)/|v|^2, t_F = 2 (N-A)·v/|v|^2. And v1 = v_x, v2 = v_y.

We might also express a = y_A, and h = x_A.

Let's denote:

Let S = |v|^2 = v1^2 + v2^2.

Then t_E = -2 (h v1 + a v2)/S. And t_F = 2 ((d - h) v1 - a v2)/S.

Now, compute the coefficients in the linear system.

Equation (1): t_E v1 X_x + (2a + t_E v2) X_y = 0.
Equation (2): t_F v1 X_x + (2a + t_F v2) X_y = (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2.

We can solve for X_x, X_y using Cramer's rule.

First, compute the determinant of the coefficient matrix:

Δ = det( [ t_E v1, 2a + t_E v2; t_F v1, 2a + t_F v2 ] ) = t_E v1 (2a + t_F v2) - t_F v1 (2a + t_E v2) = v1 [ t_E (2a + t_F v2) - t_F (2a + t_E v2) ] = v1 [ 2a(t_E - t_F) + t_E t_F v2 - t_F t_E v2 ] = v1 * 2a (t_E - t_F).

So Δ = 2a v1 (t_E - t_F). Note that t_E and t_F are generally different, so Δ ≠ 0.

Now, solve for X_x:

X_x = det( [0, 2a + t_E v2; (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2, 2a + t_F v2] ) / Δ = ( 0*(2a + t_F v2) - (2a + t_E v2)*((|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2) ) / Δ = - (2a + t_E v2) ((|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2) / Δ.

Similarly, X_y = det( [ t_E v1, 0; t_F v1, (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2 ] ) / Δ = ( t_E v1 * ((|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2) - t_F v1 * 0 ) / Δ = t_E v1 ((|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2) / Δ.

Thus:

O = (X_x, X_y) = ( - (2a + t_E v2) ((|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2) / (2a v1 (t_E - t_F)), t_E v1 ((|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2) / (2a v1 (t_E - t_F)) ).

Simplify: Let D = (|F|^2 - |B|^2)/2. Then:

O_x = - (2a + t_E v2) D / (2a v1 (t_E - t_F)).
O_y = t_E v1 D / (2a v1 (t_E - t_F)) = t_E D / (2a (t_E - t_F)).

Thus O = ( - (2a + t_E v2) D / (2a v1 (t_E - t_F)), t_E D / (2a (t_E - t_F)) ).

Now, we also have H coordinates. We can compute H in terms of A, M, N. From earlier, H = (d - α (r+h), -α a) where α = d/(d+R+r). But we can also express H in terms of the vectors. Maybe we can find a relation between O and H.

We need to show that the line through H parallel to v is tangent to circle (BEF). That is equivalent to showing that the distance from O to line through H with direction v equals radius R = OB. Since O is circumcenter, OB = distance from O to B. So we need to prove:

| (O - H) × v | / |v| = |O - B|.

Square both sides: |O-H|^2 |v|^2 - ((O-H)·v)^2 = |v|^2 |O-B|^2.

This is equivalent to: |O-H|^2 - ((O-H)·v̂)^2 = |O-B|^2.

Let’s compute O-H and O-B in terms of parameters.

We have O expressed in terms of t_E, v1, v2, a, D. And H expressed in terms of d, r, h, a. And B = (h, -a). So we can compute O-B.

Given the complexity, perhaps we can simplify using the fact that v is related to A and N? Maybe we can find that D = |F|^2 - |B|^2 over 2 can be expressed in terms of t_F and something. Since F = A + t_F v, we have |F|^2 = |A|^2 + 2 t_F A·v + t_F^2 |v|^2. And |B|^2 = |A|^2. So |F|^2 - |B|^2 = 2 t_F A·v + t_F^2 |v|^2. So D = t_F A·v + (t_F^2/2) |v|^2.

But A·v = h v1 + a v2. And t_F = 2 ((d-h)v1 - a v2)/S. So D = t_F (h v1 + a v2) + (t_F^2/2) S.

Now, note that t_E = -2 (A·v)/S. So t_E = -2 (h v1 + a v2)/S. So t_E v1 = -2 (h v1 + a v2) v1/S. And t_E v2 = -2 (h v1 + a v2) v2/S.

Also, t_E - t_F = -2(A·v)/S - 2((d-h)v1 - a v2)/S = -2[ (h v1 + a v2) + ((d-h)v1 - a v2) ]/S = -2[ h v1 + a v2 + (d-h)v1 - a v2 ]/S = -2[ d v1 ]/S = -2d v1/S.

Thus t_E - t_F = -2d v1/S. Good simplification! That's nice.

So we have:

Δ = 2a v1 (t_E - t_F) = 2a v1 * (-2d v1/S) = -4a d v1^2 / S.

Now, also compute (2a + t_E v2). We have t_E v2 = -2 (h v1 + a v2) v2/S. So 2a + t_E v2 = 2a - 2 (h v1 + a v2) v2/S = 2( a - (h v1 + a v2) v2/S ). Not sure.

Now, O_x = - (2a + t_E v2) D / (2a v1 (t_E - t_F)). But we have t_E - t_F = -2d v1/S. So 2a v1 (t_E - t_F) = 2a v1 * (-2d v1/S) = -4a d v1^2 / S. So O_x = - (2a + t_E v2) D / ( -4a d v1^2 / S ) = (2a + t_E v2) D S / (4a d v1^2).

Similarly, O_y = t_E D / (2a (t_E - t_F)) = t_E D / (2a * (-2d v1/S)) = t_E D / ( -4a d v1/S ) = - t_E D S / (4a d v1).

Now, we have O = ( O_x, O_y ) = ( (2a + t_E v2) D S / (4a d v1^2), - t_E D S / (4a d v1) ).

Now, we can also compute O-B. B = (h, -a). So O-B = ( O_x - h, O_y + a ).

We also need H. H = (h_x, h_y) = (d - α (r+h), -α a) with α = d/(d+R+r). But we can also express H in terms of d, r, h, a. Since r+h =? Let's compute r+h = r + h. Also, note that from earlier, we had H = (d - d(r+h)/(d+R+r), -d a/(d+R+r)). So H_x = d - d(r+h)/(d+R+r) = d(1 - (r+h)/(d+R+r)) = d( (d+R+r - r - h)/(d+R+r) ) = d(d+R-h)/(d+R+r). And H_y = -d a/(d+R+r). So H = ( d(d+R-h)/(d+R+r), - d a/(d+R+r) ). Good.

Let’s denote coordinates:

A = (0,0)
C = (h, y_C) where y_C = y_M - r
D = (h, y_D) where y_D = y_N + R

We have:

r^2 = h^2 + y_M^2
R^2 = h^2 + y_N^2

Also, note that y_M and y_N are related to h: y_M = -√(r^2 - h^2) (since y_M<0), y_N = √(R^2 - h^2). So y_M = -a, where a = √(r^2 - h^2) > 0; and y_N = b, where b = √(R^2 - h^2) > 0. Then A=(0,0) is on circles: h^2 + a^2 = r^2, h^2 + b^2 = R^2. And B=(2h,0). Also, y_M = -a, y_N = b.

Then:

C = (h, -a - r) because y_M - r = -a - r.
D = (h, b + R).

Now, P is circumcenter of A, C, D. Since A=0, we can compute P using the formula for circumcenter of triangle with vertices at 0, z1, z2. The circumcenter is given by ( |z1|^2 z2 - |z2|^2 z1 ) / ( z1 \bar{z2} - \bar{z1} z2 )? Actually, the circumcenter of triangle with vertices at 0, z1, z2 is the point that is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. The condition |z-O| = |z1-O| = |z2-O|. For O, we have |z-O|^2 = |z1-O|^2 => |z|^2 - z\bar{O} - \bar{z}O = |z1|^2 - z1\bar{O} - \bar{z1}O. For three points, this gives linear equations. But we can also find O as the point that is the reflection of the origin across the line through the midpoint of the chord? Actually, there is known: The circumcenter of triangle with vertices at 0, z1, z2 is given by (z1|z2|^2 - z2|z1|^2) / (z1\bar{z2} - \bar{z1}z2) times something? Let's derive.

We want O such that |O| = |O - z1| = |O - z2|. From |O|^2 = |O - z1|^2 => |O|^2 = |O|^2 - O\bar{z1} - \bar{O}z1 + |z1|^2 => O\bar{z1} + \bar{O}z1 = |z1|^2. Similarly, O\bar{z2} + \bar{O}z2 = |z2|^2.

These are two linear equations in O and \bar{O}. We can solve for O. Write O = x+iy, \bar{O}=x-iy. Then the equations become:

(x+iy)(\bar{z1}) + (x-iy)z1 = |z1|^2 => x(\bar{z1}+z1) + iy(\bar{z1}-z1) = |z1|^2. Since \bar{z1}+z1 = 2 Re(z1), \bar{z1}-z1 = -2i Im(z1). So iy(\bar{z1}-z1) = iy*(-2i Im(z1)) = 2y Im(z1). So equation: 2x Re(z1) + 2y Im(z1) = |z1|^2 => x Re(z1) + y Im(z1) = |z1|^2/2.

Similarly, x Re(z2) + y Im(z2) = |z2|^2/2.

Thus O is the solution of these linear equations. So we can solve for O = (x,y) by solving the system:

x Re(z1) + y Im(z1) = |z1|^2/2.
x Re(z2) + y Im(z2) = |z2|^2/2.

So we can compute P using this.

Let’s set z1 = C = (h, y_C) = (h, -a - r). Then Re(z1)=h, Im(z1)= -a - r. |z1|^2 = h^2 + (a+r)^2 = (r^2 - a^2) + (a+r)^2? Since h^2 = r^2 - a^2. So |z1|^2 = r^2 - a^2 + a^2 + 2ar + r^2 = 2r^2 + 2ar = 2r(r+a). So |z1|^2/2 = r(r+a).

Similarly, z2 = D = (h, y_D) = (h, b+R). Then Re(z2)=h, Im(z2)= b+R. |z2|^2 = h^2 + (b+R)^2 = (R^2 - b^2) + (b+R)^2 = R^2 - b^2 + b^2 + 2bR + R^2 = 2R^2 + 2bR = 2R(R+b). So |z2|^2/2 = R(R+b).

Thus we need to solve:

x h + y (-a - r) = r(r+a)   (1)
x h + y (b+R) = R(R+b)   (2)

Subtract (1) from (2): y (b+R + a + r) = R(R+b) - r(r+a). So y (a+b+r+R) = R^2 + Rb - r^2 - ra = (R^2 - r^2) + (Rb - ra) = (R^2 - r^2) + r(b - a). Now, the circumcenter O of BEF can be found by solving:

|O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 = |O - F|^2.

From |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 gives: (O_x - 2h)^2 + O_y^2 = (O_x - t_E p_x)^2 + (O_y - t_E p_y)^2 => Expand: O_x^2 -4h O_x +4h^2 + O_y^2 = O_x^2 -2t_E p_x O_x + t_E^2 p_x^2 + O_y^2 -2t_E p_y O_y + t_E^2 p_y^2. Cancel O_x^2, O_y^2: -4h O_x +4h^2 = -2t_E p_x O_x + t_E^2 (p_x^2+p_y^2) -2t_E p_y O_y. So:

-4h O_x +4h^2 = -2t_E p_x O_x + t_E^2 |P|^2 -2t_E p_y O_y.

Bring terms: (-4h + 2t_E p_x) O_x + 2t_E p_y O_y = t_E^2 |P|^2 -4h^2.

Similarly, from |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2 gives: (-4h + 2t_F p_x) O_x + 2t_F p_y O_y = t_F^2 |P|^2 -4h^2.

Thus we have two linear equations in O_x, O_y. We can solve for O_x, O_y. Then we need to compute distance from O to line L: H + λ P. That distance is |(O - H) × P| / |P|. And we need that to equal |O - B|.

Given the time, I think a coordinate proof is acceptable. Let's try to produce a clean analytic proof using the coordinate system we set with A at origin and AB along x-axis. We have parameters h>0, a,b>0 with h^2+a^2=r^2, h^2+b^2=R^2. We have:

- A = (0,0)
- B = (2h,0)
- M = (h, -a)
- N = (h, b)
- C = (h, -a - r)
- D = (h, b + R)
- P = circumcenter of A, C, D. We derived:

y_P = (b-a)(a+b+r) / (a+b+r+R)
x_P = (a+r)(r + y_P)/h

Now, we need H, orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M and N have same x-coordinate, the altitude from P is horizontal: y = y_P. So H = (x_H, y_P) with x_H from the condition that MH ⟂ PN. Compute:

Vector PN = N - P = (h - x_P, b - y_P)
Vector MH = H - M = (x_H - h, y_P + a)

Dot: (x_H - h)(h - x_P) + (y_P + a)(b - y_P) = 0 => (x_H - h)(h - x_P) = - (y_P + a)(b - y_P)

Thus x_H = h - (y_P + a)(b - y_P)/(h - x_P). (Equation 1)

Now, the line through H parallel to AP: direction vector = P = (x_P, y_P). So line L: (x_H, y_P) + λ (x_P, y_P).

We need to prove that L is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF.

Now, compute E and F. Since A=0, line AP is ray through P. The second intersection E of line AP with Ω (circle centered M radius r) is given by:

E = t_E P, where t_E = 2 (M·P)/|P|^2.

Similarly, F = t_F P, where t_F = 2 (N·P)/|P|^2.

Now, B = (2h,0). So we have three points: B, t_E P, t_F P.

We need to find the circumcenter O of these three points.

Let’s denote:

P = (p_x, p_y) with p_x = x_P, p_y = y_P.
Let’s denote |P|^2 = p_x^2 + p_y^2.

We have t_E = 2 (M·P)/|P|^2, where M = (h, -a). So M·P = h p_x - a p_y.
Similarly, N·P = h p_x + b p_y.

Thus:

t_E = 2 (h p_x - a p_y)/|P|^2
t_F = 2 (h p_x + b p_y)/|P|^2

Now, the circumcenter O = (x_O, y_O) of triangle with vertices B=(2h,0), E=t_E P, F=t_F P.

We can use the linear equations derived earlier:

For any two points X and Y, the perpendicular bisector condition: 2O·(X - Y) = |X|^2 - |Y|^2.

We can set up two equations using B and E, and B and F.

Let’s compute:

Equation from B and E:

2O·(B - E) = |B|^2 - |E|^2.

B - E = (2h - t_E p_x, - t_E p_y).
|B|^2 = (2h)^2 = 4h^2.
|E|^2 = t_E^2 |P|^2.

Thus: 2O·(2h - t_E p_x, - t_E p_y) = 4h^2 - t_E^2 |P|^2.

Equation from B and F:

2O·(2h - t_F p_x, - t_F p_y) = 4h^2 - t_F^2 |P|^2.

We can solve for O.

Alternatively, we can find O using the fact that the circle through B, E, F is the circle with diameter something? Since B is symmetric of A across MN, and E,F are on AP, perhaps the circle (BEF) is the image of the circle with diameter something under a homothety.

Given the complexity, maybe we can compute O in terms of P and the parameters. Let's attempt to solve the linear system for O.

Let’s denote:

A1 = 2h - t_E p_x, B1 = - t_E p_y
C1 = 4h^2 - t_E^2 |P|^2

So equation: 2 (A1 x_O + B1 y_O) = C1 => A1 x_O + B1 y_O = C1/2.

Similarly, A2 = 2h - t_F p_x, B2 = - t_F p_y, C2 = 4h^2 - t_F^2 |P|^2 => A2 x_O + B2 y_O = C2/2.

We can solve using Cramer's rule.

Compute determinant Δ = A1 B2 - A2 B1.

A1 = 2h - t_E p_x, B1 = - t_E p_y
A2 = 2h - t_F p_x, B2 = - t_F p_y

So Δ = (2h - t_E p_x)(- t_F p_y) - (2h - t_F p_x)(- t_E p_y) = - t_F p_y (2h - t_E p_x) + t_E p_y (2h - t_F p_x) = p_y [ - t_F (2h - t_E p_x) + t_E (2h - t_F p_x) ] = p_y [ -2h t_F + t_E t_F p_x + 2h t_E - t_E t_F p_x ] = p_y [ 2h (t_E - t_F) ] = 2h p_y (t_E - t_F).

Thus Δ = 2h p_y (t_E - t_F).

Now, we need t_E - t_F. t_E = 2 (h p_x - a p_y)/|P|^2, t_F = 2 (h p_x + b p_y)/|P|^2. So t_E - t_F = 2 ( -a p_y - b p_y )/|P|^2 = -2 p_y (a+b)/|P|^2.

Thus Δ = 2h p_y * ( -2 p_y (a+b)/|P|^2 ) = -4h p_y^2 (a+b)/|P|^2.

Now, compute x_O:

x_O = ( (C1/2) B2 - (C2/2) B1 ) / Δ = (1/2) ( C1 B2 - C2 B1 ) / Δ.

But we need C1 B2 - C2 B1.

C1 = 4h^2 - t_E^2 |P|^2, B2 = - t_F p_y
C2 = 4h^2 - t_F^2 |P|^2, B1 = - t_E p_y

So C1 B2 - C2 B1 = (4h^2 - t_E^2 |P|^2)(- t_F p_y) - (4h^2 - t_F^2 |P|^2)(- t_E p_y) = - t_F p_y (4h^2 - t_E^2 |P|^2) + t_E p_y (4h^2 - t_F^2 |P|^2) = p_y [ - t_F (4h^2 - t_E^2 |P|^2) + t_E (4h^2 - t_F^2 |P|^2) ] = p_y [ 4h^2 (t_E - t_F) - t_F t_E^2 |P|^2 + t_E t_F^2 |P|^2 ] = p_y [ 4h^2 (t_E - t_F) + t_E t_F |P|^2 (t_F - t_E) ] = p_y [ (t_E - t_F) (4h^2 - t_E t_F |P|^2) ].

Thus C1 B2 - C2 B1 = p_y (t_E - t_F) (4h^2 - t_E t_F |P|^2).

Then x_O = (1/2) * [ p_y (t_E - t_F) (4h^2 - t_E t_F |P|^2) ] / Δ = (1/2) * p_y (t_E - t_F) (4h^2 - t_E t_F |P|^2) / (2h p_y (t_E - t_F)) = (1/2) * (4h^2 - t_E t_F |P|^2) / (2h) = (4h^2 - t_E t_F |P|^2) / (4h).

So x_O = (4h^2 - t_E t_F |P|^2) / (4h). (Equation X)

Now, compute y_O:

y_O = ( A1 (C2/2) - A2 (C1/2) ) / Δ = (1/2) ( A1 C2 - A2 C1 ) / Δ.

We have A1 = 2h - t_E p_x, C2 = 4h^2 - t_F^2 |P|^2
A2 = 2h - t_F p_x, C1 = 4h^2 - t_E^2 |P|^2

So A1 C2 - A2 C1 = (2h - t_E p_x)(4h^2 - t_F^2 |P|^2) - (2h - t_F p_x)(4h^2 - t_E^2 |P|^2).

Expand both:

First term: (2h)(4h^2) - 2h t_F^2 |P|^2 - t_E p_x (4h^2) + t_E p_x t_F^2 |P|^2 = 8h^3 - 2h t_F^2 |P|^2 - 4h^2 t_E p_x + t_E t_F^2 p_x |P|^2.

Second term: (2h)(4h^2) - 2h t_E^2 |P|^2 - t_F p_x (4h^2) + t_F p_x t_E^2 |P|^2 = 8h^3 - 2h t_E^2 |P|^2 - 4h^2 t_F p_x + t_F t_E^2 p_x |P|^2.

Subtract: (8h^3 cancels) => A1 C2 - A2 C1 = [ -2h t_F^2 |P|^2 - 4h^2 t_E p_x + t_E t_F^2 p_x |P|^2 ] - [ -2h t_E^2 |P|^2 - 4h^2 t_F p_x + t_F t_E^2 p_x |P|^2 ] = -2h t_F^2 |P|^2 - 4h^2 t_E p_x + t_E t_F^2 p_x |P|^2 + 2h t_E^2 |P|^2 + 4h^2 t_F p_x - t_F t_E^2 p_x |P|^2.

Group terms: |P|^2 terms: -2h t_F^2 |P|^2 + 2h t_E^2 |P|^2 = 2h |P|^2 (t_E^2 - t_F^2) = 2h |P|^2 (t_E - t_F)(t_E + t_F).

p_x terms: -4h^2 t_E p_x + 4h^2 t_F p_x = 4h^2 p_x (t_F - t_E) = -4h^2 p_x (t_E - t_F).

p_x |P|^2 terms: t_E t_F^2 p_x |P|^2 - t_F t_E^2 p_x |P|^2 = t_E t_F p_x |P|^2 (t_F - t_E) = - t_E t_F p_x |P|^2 (t_E - t_F).

Thus:

A1 C2 - A2 C1 = 2h |P|^2 (t_E - t_F)(t_E + t_F) - 4h^2 p_x (t_E - t_F) - t_E t_F p_x |P|^2 (t_E - t_F) = (t_E - t_F) [ 2h |P|^2 (t_E + t_F) - 4h^2 p_x - t_E t_F p_x |P|^2 ].

Now, note that t_E - t_F = -2 p_y (a+b)/|P|^2. So we can factor.

Then y_O = (1/2) * (A1 C2 - A2 C1) / Δ = (1/2) * (t_E - t_F) [ 2h |P|^2 (t_E + t_F) - 4h^2 p_x - t_E t_F p_x |P|^2 ] / Δ.

But Δ = 2h p_y (t_E - t_F). So y_O = (1/2) * (t_E - t_F) * [ ... ] / (2h p_y (t_E - t_F)) = [ ... ] / (4h p_y).

Thus y_O = [ 2h |P|^2 (t_E + t_F) - 4h^2 p_x - t_E t_F p_x |P|^2 ] / (4h p_y).

Simplify: Divide numerator by 2h? Actually, factor 2h? Let's rewrite:

Numerator N_y = 2h |P|^2 (t_E + t_F) - 4h^2 p_x - t_E t_F p_x |P|^2 = 2h |P|^2 (t_E + t_F) - p_x (4h^2 + t_E t_F |P|^2).

Then y_O = (2h |P|^2 (t_E + t_F) - p_x (4h^2 + t_E t_F |P|^2)) / (4h p_y).

Now, we also need O-B distance. B = (2h,0). So O-B = (x_O - 2h, y_O). But note that x_O = (4h^2 - t_E t_F |P|^2)/(4h). So x_O - 2h = (4h^2 - t_E t_F |P|^2 - 8h^2)/(4h) = (-4h^2 - t_E t_F |P|^2)/(4h) = -(4h^2 + t_E t_F |P|^2)/(4h).

So O-B = ( -(4h^2 + t_E t_F |P|^2)/(4h), y_O ).

Now, we also need the line L: H + λ P. We have H = (x_H, y_P). And P = (p_x, p_y). So L direction is P.

The distance from O to L is |(O - H) × P| / |P|. And we want that to equal |O - B|.

So condition: |(O - H) × P|^2 = |P|^2 |O - B|^2.

Now, O - H = (x_O - x_H, y_O - p_y). And (O-H) × P = (x_O - x_H) p_y - (y_O - p_y) p_x (since cross product in 2D: (u,v)×(w,z)= u z - v w). So:

Cross = p_y (x_O - x_H) - p_x (y_O - p_y) = p_y x_O - p_y x_H - p_x y_O + p_x p_y.

Thus Cross = p_y x_O - p_x y_O + p_x p_y - p_y x_H.

Now, note that p_y x_O - p_x y_O is something like the determinant of (P, O). That is the cross product of P and O. Also, p_x p_y is the product.

We also have x_H from earlier: x_H = h - (p_y + a)(b - p_y)/(h - p_x). That is messy.

Maybe we can find a simpler expression for x_H. Let's compute x_H using the fact that H is orthocenter. Since we have M and N vertical, the orthocenter H can also be found by intersecting the altitude from P (horizontal) with the altitude from M (line through M perpendicular to PN). But we can also use the formula for orthocenter in terms of coordinates: For triangle with vertices (x1,y1), (x2,y2), (x3,y3), the orthocenter is given by:

H = ( x1 + x2 + x3 - 2 * (something?) Actually, there is formula using the circumcenter? Not simple.

Alternatively, we can compute H by solving the linear equations from the altitudes. Since we know that y_H = p_y, and that MH ⟂ PN, we already have an expression.

Given the complexity, maybe we can find that x_H = ? Let's compute using the derived expressions for P and the parameters. We have p_x = x_P, p_y = y_P. And we know that p_x = (a+r)(r + y_P)/h. And y_P = (b-a)(a+b+r)/S.

We can also compute h - p_x. Since p_x is huge, h-p_x is negative, but okay.

Now, we might try to compute the cross product condition directly using the expressions we have, and see if it reduces to an identity using the relations between a,b,h,r,R. This is a lot of algebra, but maybe it simplifies.

Given that the problem is likely from an olympiad, there should be a synthetic solution that is more elegant. Let's try to find a synthetic proof.

We have circles Ω and Γ intersect at A and B. Let the line through the centers meet the circles at C and D (with C on Ω, D on Γ). Then triangle ACD has circumcenter P. Then AP meets Ω again at E and Γ again at F. H is orthocenter of triangle PMN.

Goal: line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circumcircle of BEF.

Observations:

- Since P is circumcenter of ACD, we have PA=PC=PD. So P is on the perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD. Thus MP ⟂ AC (since M is on perpendicular bisector of AC) and NP ⟂ AD. So MP ⟂ AC, NP ⟂ AD.

- Also, AB is the common chord, so AB ⟂ MN. So MN ⟂ AB.

- In triangle PMN, the altitude from P is perpendicular to MN, so it is parallel to AB. So PH ∥ AB.

- The altitude from M is perpendicular to PN, and since PN ⟂ AD, we have that MH ∥ AD. Similarly, NH ∥ AC.

Thus we have:

- PH ∥ AB.
- MH ∥ AD.
- NH ∥ AC.

Now, consider the circumcircle of triangle BEF. Since B is the reflection of A across MN (line of centers), and E,F lie on line AP, maybe we can relate the circle (BEF) to the circle through A and the reflections of E,F across MN? But we already noted that reflection of E across MN is t_E P' (where P' is reflection of P across MN). That point lies on line BP'. So the circle through B, E, F has a reflection across MN that is a circle through A, t_E P', t_F P'. Not sure if that helps.

Another idea: Use the fact that the orthocenter H of triangle PMN is the center of the circle that is the reflection of something. There is a known fact: If two circles intersect, then the orthocenter of the triangle formed by the centers and the circumcenter of triangle formed by the intersection points and the far points lies on the radical axis? Not sure.

Perhaps we can use coordinate geometry but with a simpler parameterization: Let’s set up using the angle of the common chord. Since we have A at origin and AB on x-axis, the circles have equations:

Ω: (x - h)^2 + (y + a)^2 = r^2, with h^2 + a^2 = r^2. So equation: x^2 - 2hx + h^2 + y^2 + 2ay + a^2 = r^2 => x^2 + y^2 - 2hx + 2ay + (h^2+a^2) = r^2 => x^2+y^2 -2hx + 2ay = 0 (since h^2+a^2=r^2). So Ω: x^2+y^2 - 2hx + 2ay = 0. Similarly, Γ: (x - h)^2 + (y - b)^2 = R^2, with h^2+b^2=R^2. So equation: x^2 -2hx + h^2 + y^2 -2by + b^2 = R^2 => x^2+y^2 -2hx -2by = 0. So Γ: x^2+y^2 - 2hx - 2by = 0.

Thus the circles are: Ω: x^2+y^2 - 2hx + 2ay = 0; Γ: x^2+y^2 - 2hx - 2by = 0.

This is a nice symmetric representation. A=(0,0) satisfies both. B=(2h,0) satisfies both? Plug into Ω: (2h)^2+0 -2h(2h)+2a*0=4h^2-4h^2=0; Γ: 4h^2 -2h(2h)-2b*0=0. Yes.

Now, C is intersection of Ω with line MN (vertical line x=h). So set x=h in Ω: h^2+y^2 -2h^2+2ay=0 => y^2+2ay - h^2=0 => (y+a)^2 = h^2+a^2 = r^2 => y = -a ± r. Since C is below M, and M=(h,-a), we have C = (h, -a - r) (the lower intersection). D is intersection of Γ with x=h: h^2+y^2 -2h^2 -2by=0 => y^2 -2by - h^2=0 => (y-b)^2 = h^2+b^2 = R^2 => y = b ± R. Since D is above N, we have D = (h, b+R). Good.

Now, P is circumcenter of A=(0,0), C=(h, -a-r), D=(h, b+R). We can compute P using geometry.

Since A is origin, the circumcenter of triangle with vertices at 0, z1, z2 is given by: P = (|z1|^2 z2 - |z2|^2 z1) / (z1 \bar{z2} - \bar{z1} z2) ) times something? Actually, we derived linear equations earlier. Let's compute using coordinates.

We have: For points (0,0), (h, y_C), (h, y_D). The perpendicular bisector of A and C: line through midpoint (h/2, y_C/2) perpendicular to AC. AC vector = (h, y_C). So perpendicular slope = -h/y_C? Actually, line AC: slope = y_C/h. So perpendicular slope = -h/y_C. Equation: y - y_C/2 = (-h/y_C)(x - h/2). Similarly, perpendicular bisector of A and D: slope = -h/y_D, through (h/2, y_D/2). Intersection of these two lines gives P. Alternatively, we can find P as the solution of:

x h + y y_C = (h^2 + y_C^2)/2   (from |P|^2 = |P - C|^2)
x h + y y_D = (h^2 + y_D^2)/2

So we have:

h x + y_C y = (h^2+y_C^2)/2
h x + y_D y = (h^2+y_D^2)/2

Subtract: (y_D - y_C) y = (y_D^2 - y_C^2)/2 = (y_D - y_C)(y_D+y_C)/2 => y = (y_D+y_C)/2. So y_P = (y_D + y_C)/2.

Thus P has y-coordinate = (y_C + y_D)/2 = ( (-a-r) + (b+R) )/2 = (b - a + R - r)/2.

Then from first equation: h x = (h^2+y_C^2)/2 - y_C y = (h^2+y_C^2)/2 - y_C * (y_D+y_C)/2 = (h^2 + y_C^2 - y_C y_D - y_C^2)/2 = (h^2 - y_C y_D)/2. So x = (h^2 - y_C y_D)/(2h).

Now, y_C = -a - r, y_D = b + R. So y_C y_D = (-a-r)(b+R) = -(a+r)(b+R). So h^2 - y_C y_D = h^2 + (a+r)(b+R). Thus x_P = (h^2 + (a+r)(b+R)) / (2h). And y_P = (b - a + R - r)/2.

Now, note that we also have h^2 + a^2 = r^2, so h^2 = r^2 - a^2. And h^2 + b^2 = R^2, so h^2 = R^2 - b^2. So x_P = (r^2 - a^2 + (a+r)(b+R)) / (2h) = (r^2 - a^2 + ab + aR + rb + rR) / (2h). Also, y_P = (b - a + R - r)/2.

Now, H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. M = (h, -a), N = (h, b), P = (x_P, y_P). Since M and N have same x-coordinate, the altitude from P is horizontal: y = y_P. So H = (x_H, y_P) with x_H from the condition that MH ⟂ PN.

Compute PN = N - P = (h - x_P, b - y_P). MH = H - M = (x_H - h, y_P + a). Dot: (x_H - h)(h - x_P) + (y_P + a)(b - y_P) = 0 => (x_H - h)(h - x_P) = - (y_P + a)(b - y_P) => x_H = h - (y_P + a)(b - y_P)/(h - x_P). (Equation 1)

Now, the line through H parallel to AP: AP is line from A(0,0) to P(x_P,y_P). So direction vector = (x_P, y_P). So line L: (x_H, y_P) + λ (x_P, y_P).

Now, E is second intersection of line AP with Ω. Since A=0, line AP: param t P. For Ω: x^2+y^2 - 2hx + 2ay = 0. Plug in tP = (t x_P, t y_P). Then:

(t x_P)^2 + (t y_P)^2 - 2h (t x_P) + 2a (t y_P) = 0 => t^2 (x_P^2+y_P^2) - 2h t x_P + 2a t y_P = 0 => t ( t |P|^2 - 2h x_P + 2a y_P ) = 0. So t=0 gives A, and t = (2h x_P - 2a y_P)/|P|^2 = 2 (h x_P - a y_P)/|P|^2. So E = t_E P with t_E = 2 (h x_P - a y_P)/|P|^2.

Similarly, for Γ: x^2+y^2 - 2hx - 2by = 0. Plug in tP: t^2 |P|^2 - 2h t x_P - 2b t y_P = 0 => t ( t |P|^2 - 2h x_P - 2b y_P ) = 0 => t_F = 2 (h x_P + b y_P)/|P|^2.

Thus E = t_E P, F = t_F P.

Now, B = (2h,0). So we have three points: B, t_E P, t_F P.

We need to prove that L is tangent to circle (BEF). This is equivalent to showing that the power of H with respect to the circle (BEF) equals something? Actually, if a line through H is tangent, then H is a point on the tangent line. The condition for a line through H to be tangent to a circle is that the distance from the center to the line equals the radius. That is a metric condition.

But maybe we can use the fact that the circle (BEF) is the image of the line through A under some inversion? Alternatively, we can compute the circumcenter O of BEF and then compute the distance condition.

Given the symmetric forms, we can try to compute O in terms of P and the parameters. Using the same linear equations as before but with these parameters.

We have B=(2h,0), E=t_E P, F=t_F P.

We already derived formulas for x_O and y_O in terms of t_E, t_F, P, h. Let's compute them with the expressions we have.

From earlier: x_O = (4h^2 - t_E t_F |P|^2) / (4h). And y_O = [2h |P|^2 (t_E + t_F) - p_x (4h^2 + t_E t_F |P|^2)] / (4h p_y) (since we had y_O = [2h |P|^2 (t_E + t_F) - p_x (4h^2 + t_E t_F |P|^2)] / (4h p_y)). But careful: In our earlier derivation, we had p_y = y_P. And we had y_O = [2h |P|^2 (t_E + t_F) - p_x (4h^2 + t_E t_F |P|^2)] / (4h p_y). That seems correct.

Now, we also have O-B = (x_O - 2h, y_O). Compute x_O - 2h = (4h^2 - t_E t_F |P|^2)/(4h) - 2h = (4h^2 - t_E t_F |P|^2 - 8h^2)/(4h) = -(4h^2 + t_E t_F |P|^2)/(4h). So O-B = ( - (4h^2 + t_E t_F |P|^2)/(4h), y_O ).

Now, we need to compute the distance from O to line L: L is through H with direction P. The distance d = |(O - H) × P| / |P|.

We have H = (x_H, y_P). And P = (p_x, p_y) = (x_P, y_P). So O - H = (x_O - x_H, y_O - y_P). Then cross = (x_O - x_H) p_y - (y_O - y_P) p_x.

We want to show that d = |O - B|. Square both sides: |(O-H) × P|^2 = |P|^2 |O-B|^2.

Now, we can try to compute these using the expressions we have, and see if they simplify to an identity using the relations between a,b,h,r,R. This is a lot of algebra but maybe we can find a clever substitution.

Let's compute t_E and t_F in terms of x_P, y_P, a, b, h. We have:

t_E = 2 (h x_P - a y_P)/|P|^2.
t_F = 2 (h x_P + b y_P)/|P|^2.

Then t_E + t_F = 2 (2h x_P + (b - a) y_P)/|P|^2 = (4h x_P + 2(b-a) y_P)/|P|^2.
t_E t_F = 4 (h x_P - a y_P)(h x_P + b y_P)/|P|^4 = 4 (h^2 x_P^2 + h x_P (b - a) y_P - a b y_P^2)/|P|^4.

Now, |P|^2 = x_P^2 + y_P^2.

Now, we also have expressions for x_P and y_P in terms of a,b,h,r,R. Let's derive them more clearly.

We have:

y_P = (b - a + R - r)/2.   (1)
x_P = (h^2 + (a+r)(b+R))/(2h).   (2)

Also, from circle equations: h^2 + a^2 = r^2, h^2 + b^2 = R^2. So we can express r and R in terms of h,a,b: r = √(h^2+a^2), R = √(h^2+b^2). But maybe we can keep r and R.

Now, we also have the relation between a, b, h, r, R. Not independent.

Now, we need to compute x_H from (1). x_H = h - (y_P + a)(b - y_P)/(h - x_P). Let's compute y_P + a and b - y_P.

y_P = (b - a + R - r)/2. So y_P + a = (b - a + R - r + 2a)/2 = (b + a + R - r)/2 = (a+b+R-r)/2.
b - y_P = b - (b - a + R - r)/2 = (2b - b + a - R + r)/2 = (b + a - R + r)/2 = (a+b - R + r)/2.

Thus (y_P + a)(b - y_P) = (a+b+R-r)(a+b-R+r)/4 = [ (a+b)^2 - (R-r)^2 ]/4? Actually, (X+Y)(X-Y)=X^2-Y^2 where X = a+b, Y = R-r? Let's compute: (a+b+R-r)(a+b-R+r) = (a+b)^2 - (R-r)^2? Because (a+b+R-r)(a+b-R+r) = ((a+b)+(R-r))((a+b)-(R-r)) = (a+b)^2 - (R-r)^2. So product = [(a+b)^2 - (R-r)^2]/4.

Now, h - x_P. Compute x_P from (2): x_P = (h^2 + (a+r)(b+R))/(2h). So h - x_P = h - (h^2 + (a+r)(b+R))/(2h) = (2h^2 - h^2 - (a+r)(b+R))/(2h) = (h^2 - (a+r)(b+R))/(2h). So h - x_P = (h^2 - (a+r)(b+R))/(2h). (Note: h^2 is likely less than (a+r)(b+R) because x_P > h? In numeric, x_P=15.8, h=0.25, so h-x_P negative. So denominator negative.)

Thus x_H = h - ( (a+b+R-r)(a+b-R+r)/4 ) / ( (h^2 - (a+r)(b+R))/(2h) ) = h - ( (a+b+R-r)(a+b-R+r) )/(4) * (2h)/(h^2 - (a+r)(b+R)) = h - (h (a+b+R-r)(a+b-R+r))/(2 (h^2 - (a+r)(b+R))).

So x_H = h [ 1 - ( (a+b+R-r)(a+b-R+r) )/(2 (h^2 - (a+r)(b+R)) ].

Now, we also have O-B coordinates: O-B = ( - (4h^2 + t_E t_F |P|^2)/(4h), y_O ). And y_O we have expression. Maybe we can compute (O-H) × P in terms of knowns and then compare with |P|^2 |O-B|^2.

Given the complexity, perhaps a coordinate proof is acceptable. I'll try to produce a clean coordinate proof using the symmetric coordinates we derived.

We have:

- A = (0,0)
- B = (2h,0)
- M = (h, -a)
- N = (h, b)
- C = (h, -a-r)
- D = (h, b+R)
- P = (x_P, y_P) with y_P = (b - a + R - r)/2, x_P = (h^2 + (a+r)(b+R))/(2h).

Now, we need to prove: line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF). We can compute the equation of circle (BEF) and then check the condition.

First, compute t_E and t_F:

t_E = 2 (h x_P - a y_P)/|P|^2.
t_F = 2 (h x_P + b y_P)/|P|^2.

Now, E = t_E P, F = t_F P.

Let’s compute the circumcircle of B, E, F. Since B, E, F are collinear? No, B is on x-axis, E and F are on line through origin (AP). So they are not collinear unless P is horizontal. So we can find the circle.

Alternatively, we can find the circumcenter O by solving the linear equations. We already have x_O and y_O in terms of t_E, t_F, h, P.

Let's compute these explicitly using the expressions for x_P and y_P.

First, compute |P|^2 = x_P^2 + y_P^2. We have x_P and y_P in terms of a,b,h,r,R. But maybe we can find simpler forms using the circle equations.

We have h^2 + a^2 = r^2, h^2 + b^2 = R^2. So r^2 = h^2 + a^2, R^2 = h^2 + b^2. Then we can express r and R as functions of h,a,b. But keep them as r,R for now.

We can also compute (a+r)(b+R). That appears in x_P. Let's compute x_P explicitly:

x_P = (h^2 + (a+r)(b+R))/(2h).

Now, compute h x_P - a y_P and h x_P + b y_P.

First, h x_P = (h^2 + (a+r)(b+R))/2.

So h x_P - a y_P = (h^2 + (a+r)(b+R))/2 - a y_P.
And h x_P + b y_P = (h^2 + (a+r)(b+R))/2 + b y_P.

Now, y_P = (b - a + R - r)/2.

Thus a y_P = a (b - a + R - r)/2 = (ab - a^2 + aR - ar)/2.
b y_P = b (b - a + R - r)/2 = (b^2 - ab + bR - br)/2.

Now, (a+r)(b+R) = ab + aR + rb + rR.

So h^2 + (a+r)(b+R) = h^2 + ab + aR + rb + rR.

Thus h x_P = (h^2 + ab + aR + rb + rR)/2.

Now, compute h x_P - a y_P = (h^2 + ab + aR + rb + rR)/2 - (ab - a^2 + aR - ar)/2 = [h^2 + ab + aR + rb + rR - ab + a^2 - aR + ar]/2 = [h^2 + a^2 + rb + rR + ar]/2 = [ (h^2+a^2) + rb + rR + ar ]/2 = [r^2 + r(b + R + a)]/2 = r ( r + a + b + R )/2.

Thus h x_P - a y_P = r (a+b+r+R)/2 = r S/2, where S = a+b+r+R.

Similarly, h x_P + b y_P = (h^2 + ab + aR + rb + rR)/2 + (b^2 - ab + bR - br)/2 = [h^2 + ab + aR + rb + rR + b^2 - ab + bR - br]/2 = [h^2 + b^2 + aR + rb + rR + bR - br]/2 = [R^2 + rR + aR + rb + rR - br]/2? Wait, h^2+b^2 = R^2, so numerator = R^2 + aR + rb + rR + bR - br = R^2 + aR + bR + rb + rR - br. But rb - br = 0. So numerator = R^2 + (a+b)R + rR = R(R + a + b) + rR = R(R+a+b) + rR = R(S - r) + rR? Actually, S = a+b+r+R, so R+a+b = S - r. So numerator = R(S - r) + rR = RS - rR + rR = RS. So h x_P + b y_P = RS/2.

Thus we have nice simplifications:

t_E = 2 (h x_P - a y_P)/|P|^2 = 2 * (r S/2) / |P|^2 = r S / |P|^2.
t_F = 2 (h x_P + b y_P)/|P|^2 = 2 * (RS/2) / |P|^2 = R S / |P|^2.

So t_E = r S / |P|^2, t_F = R S / |P|^2. That's neat.

Thus E = (r S / |P|^2) P, F = (R S / |P|^2) P.

Now, B = (2h,0). And P = (x_P, y_P) with x_P = (h^2 + (a+r)(b+R))/(2h) and y_P = (b - a + R - r)/2.

We also have |P|^2 = x_P^2 + y_P^2.

Now, compute x_O and y_O using these.

From earlier: x_O = (4h^2 - t_E t_F |P|^2) / (4h) = (4h^2 - (r S / |P|^2)*(R S / |P|^2)*|P|^2) / (4h) = (4h^2 - r R S^2 / |P|^2) / (4h). That's not as simple.

Maybe we can find O by noting that the circle through B, E, F is the circle with diameter the point where the line through B perpendicular to AP meets AP? Let's examine.

Consider the circle through B and two points on line through origin. The center O lies on the perpendicular bisector of EF, which is a line perpendicular to AP (since AP is the line through origin and P, and EF lies on AP). So the perpendicular bisector of EF is a line through the midpoint of EF, perpendicular to AP. Also, O lies on the perpendicular bisector of B and one of the points, say E. So O is the intersection of these two lines.

We can compute the midpoint of EF: ( (α+β)/2 * P ) = ( (α+β)/2 * (x_P, y_P). The line perpendicular to AP through this midpoint has direction perpendicular to P. So its equation is: (x - (α+β)x_P/2, y - (α+β)y_P/2) dot (x_P, y_P) = 0. So (x_P)(x - (α+β)x_P/2) + y_P(y - (α+β)y_P/2) = 0 => x_P x + y_P y = (α+β)/2 (x_P^2+y_P^2) = (α+β)|P|^2/2.

Thus the perpendicular bisector of EF is line: x_P x + y_P y = (α+β)|P|^2/2.

Now, the perpendicular bisector of B and E: B=(2h,0), E=αP=(α x_P, α y_P). Midpoint M_{BE} = ((2h+α x_P)/2, α y_P/2). The slope of BE: (α y_P - 0)/(α x_P - 2h) = α y_P/(α x_P - 2h). The perpendicular slope is - (α x_P - 2h)/(α y_P). So the equation of perpendicular bisector of BE: y - α y_P/2 = - (α x_P - 2h)/(α y_P) (x - (2h+α x_P)/2).

We can also find the linear equation by using the condition that O is equidistant from B and E: |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 => 2O·(B-E) = |B|^2 - |E|^2. B-E = (2h - α x_P, -α y_P). |B|^2 = 4h^2. |E|^2 = α^2|P|^2. So equation: 2( (2h - α x_P)x_O + (-α y_P) y_O ) = 4h^2 - α^2|P|^2 => (2h - α x_P)x_O - α y_P y_O = 2h^2 - (α^2/2)|P|^2. This is one linear equation.

Similarly, from B and F: (2h - β x_P)x_O - β y_P y_O = 2h^2 - (β^2/2)|P|^2.

Now, we also have the equation from the perpendicular bisector of EF: x_P x_O + y_P y_O = (α+β)|P|^2/2.

So we have three linear equations in x_O, y_O. We can solve for x_O, y_O. We already have a formula for x_O from earlier that involved t_E, t_F. But maybe we can find a nicer expression for O.

Let's denote:

Equation (1): (2h - α x_P) x_O - α y_P y_O = 2h^2 - (α^2/2) |P|^2.
Equation (2): (2h - β x_P) x_O - β y_P y_O = 2h^2 - (β^2/2) |P|^2.
Equation (3): x_P x_O + y_P y_O = (α+β)|P|^2/2.

We can solve using (3) and one of the others. Let's try to find O.

From (3): y_P y_O = (α+β)|P|^2/2 - x_P x_O.

Substitute into (1):

(2h - α x_P) x_O - α [ (α+β)|P|^2/2 - x_P x_O ] = 2h^2 - (α^2/2) |P|^2.
=> (2h - α x_P) x_O - α(α+β)|P|^2/2 + α x_P x_O = 2h^2 - (α^2/2) |P|^2.
=> (2h - α x_P + α x_P) x_O = 2h^2 - (α^2/2) |P|^2 + α(α+β)|P|^2/2.
=> 2h x_O = 2h^2 - (α^2/2) |P|^2 + (α^2+αβ)|P|^2/2 = 2h^2 + (αβ)|P|^2/2.
Thus x_O = (2h^2 + αβ|P|^2/2) / (2h) = h + (αβ|P|^2)/(4h).

So x_O = h + (αβ|P|^2)/(4h). That's a nice expression.

Similarly, we can get y_O from (3) and (2). Let's do it.

From (3): x_P x_O = (α+β)|P|^2/2 - y_P y_O.

Substitute into (2):

(2h - β x_P) x_O - β y_P y_O = 2h^2 - (β^2/2) |P|^2.
But we can also use the same approach: Solve for y_O directly.

We have from (1) we got x_O. Now plug into (3) to get y_O:

Let's define:

q = (b-a+R-r)/2.

Let S = a+b+r+R.

Define α = rS/|P|^2, β = RS/|P|^2. Then E = αP, F = βP.

Now, the circumcenter O of triangle BEF (B = (2h,0)) can be found by solving the linear system:

(1) (x_O - 2h)^2 + y_O^2 = (x_O - αp)^2 + (y_O - αq)^2,
(2) (x_O - 2h)^2 + y_O^2 = (x_O - βp)^2 + (y_O - βq)^2.

These give linear equations:

2x_O(2h - αp) + 2y_O(0 - αq) = 4h^2 - α^2(p^2+q^2),
2x_O(2h - βp) + 2y_O(0 - βq) = 4h^2 - β^2(p^2+q^2).

Simplify: (2h - αp)x_O - αq y_O = 2h^2 - (α^2/2)|P|^2,
(2h - βp)x_O - βq y_O = 2h^2 - (β^2/2)|P|^2.

Using α = rS/|P|^2, β = RS/|P|^2, we can simplify. Let’s denote T = |P|^2.

Then α^2/2 = r^2 S^2/(2T^2), and αp = (rS/T) p. So the equations become:

(2h - (rS/T)p) x_O - (rS/T) q y_O = 2h^2 - (r^2 S^2/(2T^2)) T = 2h^2 - (r^2 S^2)/(2T).
Similarly, (2h - (RS/T)p) x_O - (RS/T) q y_O = 2h^2 - (R^2 S^2)/(2T).

Multiply each equation by T to avoid denominators:

(2hT - rS p) x_O - rS q y_O = 2h^2 T - (r^2 S^2)/2,
(2hT - RS p) x_O - RS q y_O = 2h^2 T - (R^2 S^2)/2.

Now, we also have the equation of perpendicular bisector of EF: (x_O - αp) p + (y_O - αq) q =? Actually, β^2-α^2 = (β-α)(β+α). So dividing by (β-α) (nonzero) gives: x_O p + y_O q = ((β+α)T)/2 = ((r+R)S/2) T / T? Wait, β+α = (r+R)S/T. So ((β+α)T)/2 = ((r+R)S)/2. So we have:

p x_O + q y_O = ((r+R)S)/2.   (Equation (A))

Thus (A) is the equation from the perpendicular bisector of EF.

Now, we also have from the equations for O from B and E, we can solve for x_O and y_O. But we already have (A). We need another linear equation. We can use the equation from B and E, but perhaps we can also derive from the fact that O is equidistant from B and E. That gives:

(x_O - 2h)^2 + y_O^2 = (x_O - αp)^2 + (y_O - αq)^2 => Expand and cancel: -4h x_O +4h^2 + y_O^2 = -2αp x_O -2αq y_O + α^2 T? Actually, careful: (x_O-2h)^2+y_O^2 = x_O^2 -4h x_O+4h^2+y_O^2. (x_O-αp)^2+(y_O-αq)^2 = x_O^2 -2αp x_O+α^2 p^2 + y_O^2 -2αq y_O+α^2 q^2 = x_O^2+y_O^2 -2α(p x_O+q y_O)+α^2 T. Set equal: -4h x_O+4h^2 = -2α(p x_O+q y_O)+α^2 T => 4h x_O -4h^2 = 2α(p x_O+q y_O)-α^2 T => (4h - 2α p)x_O - 2α q y_O = 4h^2 - α^2 T. Multiply by 1/2: (2h - α p)x_O - α q y_O = 2h^2 - (α^2/2)T. Now, we have (A): p x_O + q y_O = ((r+R)S)/2.

We can solve these three linear equations. Let's use (A) and the B-E equation. Write:

(2h - α p)x_O - α q y_O = 2h^2 - (α^2/2)T.   (B1)

From (A): p x_O + q y_O = ((r+R)S)/2. Multiply (A) by α: α p x_O + α q y_O = α((r+R)S)/2 = ((r+R)S α)/2. Then add to (B1): (2h - α p + α p)x_O + (-α q + α q)y_O = 2h^2 - (α^2/2)T + α((r+R)S)/2 => 2h x_O = 2h^2 + α((r+R)S - α T)/2? Actually, α((r+R)S)/2 - (α^2/2)T = (α/2)((r+R)S - α T). But α = rS/T, so α T = rS. Thus (r+R)S - α T = (r+R)S - rS = R S. So α((r+R)S - α T)/2 = (α/2)(R S) = (rS/T)(R S)/2 = (r R S^2)/(2T). Thus 2h x_O = 2h^2 + (r R S^2)/(2T). So x_O = h + (r R S^2)/(4h T). That's (2').

We have triangle ACD. Its circumcenter P. Then line AP meets Ω again at E and Γ again at F. So E is second intersection of AP with Ω, F second intersection with Γ. So line AP is the same as line AEF. P lies somewhere on that line.

Now H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. So H is intersection of altitudes from P to MN, from M to PN, from N to PM.

Goal: line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circumcircle of BEF.

We need to prove that.

Observation: Since P is circumcenter of ACD, we have PA = PC = PD. So P lies on perpendicular bisectors of AC, AD, CD. Also, A, C, D are points on circles? A is intersection of Ω and Γ, C on Ω, D on Γ. So A, C are on Ω, A, D on Γ.

We have E on Ω and on line AP. So E is the second intersection of line AP with Ω. Similarly, F is second intersection with Γ.

We can try to find relationships. Perhaps some known configurations: For two intersecting circles, the line through the centers meets the circles at points; the circumcenter of triangle formed by one intersection and the two far points is related to the radical axis, etc. Also, orthocenter of triangle PMN might have some property.

Let me denote coordinates or use inversion? Might be easier to set up coordinate system.

Let's place M and N on x-axis. Let M = (0,0) and N = (d,0) with d > 0. The circles: Ω: center M radius r, equation x^2+y^2 = r^2. Γ: center N radius R, equation (x-d)^2 + y^2 = R^2. Intersection points A and B satisfy both. Since r < R, and they intersect at two points, we have |d - r| < R? Actually intersection condition: distance between centers d satisfies |R - r| < d < R+r. Since r<R, we have d > R-r, and d < R+r.

We have points C and D on line MN (x-axis) such that C is on Ω and order C, M, N, D. Since M at 0, N at d>0, and C is left of M (since order C, M, N, D: so C is to the left of M, M between C and N). So C = (-r,0) because on Ω, the point on x-axis left of M is (-r,0). Similarly, D is on Γ on the right side of N, so D = (d+R,0) because the intersection of Γ with x-axis to the right of N is (d+R,0). But check order: C (-r,0), M (0,0), N (d,0), D (d+R,0). That's correct if -r < 0 < d < d+R. So C = (-r,0), D = (d+R,0). Good.

Now A is an intersection point of the circles. We can compute coordinates. Let A = (x_A, y_A) with y_A > 0 (choose the upper intersection). Then A satisfies x_A^2 + y_A^2 = r^2 and (x_A-d)^2 + y_A^2 = R^2. Subtract: (x_A-d)^2 - x_A^2 = R^2 - r^2 => (x_A^2 -2dx_A + d^2) - x_A^2 = -2dx_A + d^2 = R^2 - r^2 => -2dx_A + d^2 = R^2 - r^2 => 2dx_A = d^2 - (R^2 - r^2) => x_A = (d^2 - (R^2 - r^2))/(2d) = (d^2 - R^2 + r^2)/(2d). Then y_A = sqrt(r^2 - x_A^2) (positive). Similarly, B is the lower intersection (x_A, -y_A).

Now triangle ACD: A (x_A, y_A), C (-r,0), D (d+R,0). Its circumcenter P. We can compute P. Since C and D lie on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line through midpoint of CD. Midpoint of C and D: ((-r + d+R)/2, 0). So the perpendicular bisector of CD is x = (d+R - r)/2. Also, P lies on perpendicular bisector of AC and AD. So P is intersection of x = (d+R - r)/2 with perpendicular bisector of AC (or AD). So we can find P.

Let's denote p_x = (d+R - r)/2. Then we need p_y such that P is equidistant from A and C (or A and D). Compute distances squared: PA^2 = (p_x - x_A)^2 + (p_y - y_A)^2; PC^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2. Set equal:

(p_x - x_A)^2 + (p_y - y_A)^2 = (p_x + r)^2 + p_y^2

=> (p_x^2 -2p_x x_A + x_A^2) + (p_y^2 -2p_y y_A + y_A^2) = (p_x^2 + 2r p_x + r^2) + p_y^2

Cancel p_x^2, p_y^2: -2p_x x_A + x_A^2 -2p_y y_A + y_A^2 = 2r p_x + r^2

=> -2p_x x_A -2p_y y_A + (x_A^2+y_A^2) = 2r p_x + r^2

But x_A^2+y_A^2 = r^2 (since A on Ω). So:

-2p_x x_A -2p_y y_A + r^2 = 2r p_x + r^2 => -2p_x x_A -2p_y y_A = 2r p_x => Divide 2: -p_x x_A - p_y y_A = r p_x => So p_y y_A = -p_x x_A - r p_x = -p_x (x_A + r) => p_y = -p_x (x_A + r)/y_A.

Since y_A >0, p_y is negative if x_A+r positive? x_A is maybe between -r and r? Actually A is on Ω, so x_A is between -r and r. x_A+r >=0. So p_y negative. So P lies below x-axis. Good.

Thus P = (p_x, p_y) with p_y = -p_x (x_A+r)/y_A.

Now line AP: A (x_A,y_A) and P (p_x,p_y). Its equation. Then E is second intersection of this line with Ω (circle centered M radius r). Since A is on Ω, E is the other intersection. Similarly, F is second intersection with Γ.

We can compute E and F parametrically. Let’s find the coordinates of E. For a line through A, we can write param: A + t*(P-A). For t=0 gives A, for t=1 gives P. We want the other intersection with Ω: solve for t such that |A + t(P-A)|^2 = r^2. Since A satisfies, we have quadratic in t. The product of roots =? Actually we can use that if a line through A intersects circle at A and E, then the chord AE satisfies that the midpoint of AE lies on the line from M perpendicular to chord. But we can compute t_E =? Using the fact that for a circle, if we have a point A on circle, and a direction vector v, then the other intersection is given by A + t v where t = -2 (A·v)/|v|^2? Wait: For circle centered at origin, points on circle satisfy |x|^2 = r^2. If A is on circle, then for line A+ t v, we have |A+ t v|^2 = r^2 => |A|^2 + 2t A·v + t^2|v|^2 = r^2 => since |A|^2 = r^2, we get 2t A·v + t^2|v|^2=0 => t(2A·v + t|v|^2)=0. So t=0 (A) and t = -2(A·v)/|v|^2. So E = A + t_E v, where v = P-A. So t_E = -2(A·(P-A))/|P-A|^2 = -2(A·P - |A|^2)/|P-A|^2 = -2(A·P - r^2)/|P-A|^2.

Similarly, for Γ: center N = (d,0). Let w = P-A. Then line: A + t w. Intersection with Γ: |A + t w - N|^2 = R^2. Since A is on Γ, we have |A-N|^2 = R^2. So similar: t(2(A-N)·w + t|w|^2)=0 => t=0 (A) and t = -2((A-N)·w)/|w|^2. So F = A + t_F w, where t_F = -2((A-N)·w)/|w|^2.

Thus we have coordinates for E and F.

Now we have triangle BEF. Its circumcircle. We need to show that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to that circle. Alternate approach: Use coordinate geometry and compute everything, then show that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to (BEF). That is brute force but can be done systematically with symbolic algebra. Since it's an olympiad problem, there should be a synthetic proof. But as a proof generator, we can produce a coordinate proof that is rigorous. Let's attempt that.

Set up coordinates as above: M=(0,0), N=(d,0) with d>0, r<R, and d satisfies |R-r| < d < R+r. C=(-r,0), D=(d+R,0). A=(x_A, y_A) with y_A>0, x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d), y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2.

We have P = circumcenter of ACD. Compute P explicitly. Since C and D on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line x = ( -r + d+R )/2 = (d+R-r)/2 = p_x. So p_x = (d+R-r)/2. Then p_y from condition PA=PC. Using earlier derived: p_y = -p_x (x_A+r)/y_A. So P = (p_x, p_y).

Now line AP: direction v = P - A = (p_x - x_A, p_y - y_A). Let's compute v explicitly.

p_x - x_A = (d+R-r)/2 - (d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d) = (1/2)[ (d+R-r) - (d^2+r^2-R^2)/d ] = (1/(2d))[ d(d+R-r) - (d^2+r^2-R^2) ] = (1/(2d))[ d^2+dr -dr - d^2 - r^2 + R^2 ] = (1/(2d))[ R^2 - r^2 ]. So p_x - x_A = (R^2 - r^2)/(2d). Good.

p_y - y_A = -p_x (x_A+r)/y_A - y_A = -(p_x(x_A+r) + y_A^2)/y_A = -(p_x(x_A+r) + r^2 - x_A^2)/y_A? Since y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2. Let O = (x, y). Then:

(1) (x - 1/4)^2 + (y + s/4)^2 = (x - 7/8)^2 + (y + s/8)^2
(2) (x - 1/4)^2 + (y + s/4)^2 = (x - 3/2)^2 + (y + s/2)^2

Expand differences.

From (1): (x^2 - (1/2)x + 1/16) + (y^2 + (s/2)y + s^2/16) = (x^2 - (7/4)x + 49/64) + (y^2 + (s/4)y + s^2/64). Cancel x^2,y^2. Then:

- (1/2)x + 1/16 + (s/2)y + s^2/16 = - (7/4)x + 49/64 + (s/4)y + s^2/64.

Bring all terms: (-1/2)x + (7/4)x + (s/2)y - (s/4)y + 1/16 - 49/64 + s^2/16 - s^2/64 = 0.

Compute coefficients: x: (-2/4 + 7/4) = 5/4 x. y: (2s/4 - s/4) = s/4 y. Constant: 1/16 - 49/64 = 4/64 - 49/64 = -45/64. s^2/16 - s^2/64 = 4s^2/64 - s^2/64 = 3s^2/64. So constant = (-45 + 3s^2)/64.

But s^2 = 15, so 3*15=45. So constant = (-45+45)/64 = 0. So equation reduces to: (5/4)x + (s/4)y = 0 => 5x + s y = 0 => s y = -5x => y = -5x/s.

So (1) gives: y = -5x/√15. That is a line through origin? Indeed O lies on line through origin with slope -5/√15. But note that x0? Actually O is on that line.

Now from (2): (x - 1/4)^2 + (y + s/4)^2 = (x - 3/2)^2 + (y + s/2)^2. Expand similarly:

Left: (x^2 - (1/2)x + 1/16) + (y^2 + (s/2)y + s^2/16). Right: (x^2 - 3x + 9/4) + (y^2 + s y + s^2/4). Cancel x^2,y^2. Then:

- (1/2)x + 1/16 + (s/2)y + s^2/16 = -3x + 9/4 + s y + s^2/4.

Bring terms: (-1/2)x + 3x + (s/2)y - s y + 1/16 - 9/4 + s^2/16 - s^2/4 = 0.

Compute x: (-0.5+3)=2.5 = 5/2 x. y: (s/2 - s)= -s/2 y. Constant: 1/16 - 9/4 = 1/16 - 36/16 = -35/16. s^2/16 - s^2/4 = s^2/16 - 4s^2/16 = -3s^2/16 = -3*15/16 = -45/16. So constant = -35/16 -45/16 = -80/16 = -5. So equation: (5/2)x - (s/2)y - 5 = 0 => Multiply 2: 5x - s y - 10 = 0 => s y = 5x - 10 => y = (5x - 10)/s.

Now we have two equations for O: y = -5x/s from (1), and y = (5x - 10)/s from (2). Equate: -5x/s = (5x - 10)/s => -5x = 5x - 10 => 10x = 10 => x = 1. Then y = -5*1/s = -5/√15 = -√15/3? Actually -5/√15 = -5√15/15 = -√15/3 ≈ -1.290994. So O = (1, -√15/3). Radius squared: distance to B: (1-1/4)^2 + (-√15/3 + √15/4)^2 = (3/4)^2 + ( (-4√15/12 + 3√15/12)=(-√15/12) )^2 = (9/16) + (15/144) = (9/16) + (5/48) = (27/48 + 5/48)=32/48=2/3. So radius = √(2/3) ≈ 0.81649658. Good.

Now H = (p_x, h_y) = (3/2, - d y_A/(d+R+r) )? Actually we computed H from formula: H = (d(d+R-x_A)/(d+R+r), - d y_A/(d+R+r)). With d=2, R=2, x_A=1/4, y_A=√15/4. Then d+R-x_A = 2+2-0.25=3.75=15/4. d+R+r = 2+2+1=5. So H_x = 2*(15/4)/5 = (30/4)/5= (15/2)/5=15/10=3/2=1.5. H_y = -2*(√15/4)/5 = - (√15/2)/5 = -√15/10. So H = (3/2, -√15/10). v = (5/4, -3√15/4). So line through H parallel to v: param H + u v. We want distance from O to this line. Compute vector from H to O: O - H = (1 - 3/2, -√15/3 + √15/10) = (-1/2, (-10√15/30 + 3√15/30)= -7√15/30). So H->O = (-1/2, -7√15/30). Now cross product with v: det( H->O, v ) = (-1/2)*( -3√15/4 ) - (-7√15/30)*(5/4) = ( (1/2)*(3√15/4) ) + ( (7√15/30)*(5/4) ) = (3√15/8) + (35√15/120) = (3√15/8) + (7√15/24) = common denominator 24: (9√15/24 + 7√15/24)=16√15/24=2√15/3. So |det| = 2√15/3. |v| = √10. So distance = (2√15/3)/√10 = (2√15)/(3√10)= (2√(15/10))/3 = (2√(3/2))/3 = (2√(3)/√2)/3 = (2√3)/(3√2)= (2√6)/(6)= √6/3? Actually (2√3)/(3√2)= (2√6)/(6)= √6/3. So distance = √6/3 ≈ 0.81649658. That equals radius! Indeed radius = √(2/3) = √2/√3 = √6/3. Yes, √6/3 ≈ 0.81649658. So distance equals radius, so line is tangent. Great.

Thus in this numeric example, the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to (BEF). So the claim holds.

Now we need to prove it in general. We can attempt to compute O (circumcenter of BEF) and distance from O to line through H parallel to v, and show it equals radius.

Given that the configuration is symmetric about the x-axis? Actually, we have B as reflection of A across x-axis, and E and F lie on line AP. So maybe we can find that the circumcircle of BEF is the image of the circle through A, E, F? Not exactly.

Alternatively, we can use vector algebra and derive that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent if and only if some relation holds, which we can verify using coordinates.

Let's attempt to compute O in terms of A, v, t_E, t_F, B.

We have points: B = (x_A, -y_A). Since A = (x_A, y_A). Let’s denote A = (a_x, a_y) with a_y>0. Then B = (a_x, -a_y). v = (v_x, v_y) direction of AP. We have t_E and t_F given by:

We can also compute t_E and t_F in terms of the distances? Alternatively, we can use the fact that E is the second intersection of line AP with Ω. Since Ω is circle centered M at (0,0) radius r, and A is on Ω, we have that AE is a chord of Ω. The midpoint of AE is the projection of M onto line AP. Similarly, for F on Γ, midpoint of AF is projection of N onto line AP. So we can find t_E and t_F using the projection.

Let’s denote the foot of perpendicular from M to line AP. Since M is at origin, the foot is the projection of 0 onto line AP. That foot is some point on line AP. The chord AE is such that the line from M to midpoint of AE is perpendicular to AE. So the midpoint of AE is the projection of M onto line AP. So if we let param on line AP: A + t v, then the projection of M onto line AP is given by t0 = -A·v/|v|^2? Actually, the projection of point X onto line through A with direction v is given by: X_proj = A + ((X - A)·v)/|v|^2 v. For X=M=(0,0), then (M-A)·v = -A·v. So projection = A - (A·v)/|v|^2 v. That is the foot. The midpoint of AE is that foot. Since A and E are symmetric with respect to this foot (the foot is midpoint of chord AE because M is center). So if foot = A + t_mid v, then t_mid = -A·v/|v|^2. And E = A + t_E v, where t_E = 2 t_mid = -2A·v/|v|^2. That matches our t_E. Good.

Similarly, for Γ: center N, foot of perpendicular from N to line AP is midpoint of AF. So t_mid_N = ((N-A)·v)/|v|^2? Actually, projection of N onto line: N_proj = A + ((N - A)·v)/|v|^2 v. Then midpoint of AF = N_proj, and F = A + 2 t_mid_N v = A + 2((N-A)·v)/|v|^2 v. So t_F = 2((N-A)·v)/|v|^2. But earlier we had t_F = -2((A-N)·v)/|v|^2 = 2((N-A)·v)/|v|^2. Yes, consistent.

So we have t_E = -2 A·v/|v|^2, t_F = 2 (N-A)·v/|v|^2.

Now note that A, N, M are known. v = P-A, but we may not need P explicitly. We can find v in terms of coordinates. Because MP is perpendicular to AC. And we know M is origin, P is some point. v = P-A. But AC is chord of Ω from A to C. Since M is center, the line from M to midpoint of AC is perpendicular to AC. So AC is perpendicular to line from M to midpoint of AC. But we also have MP is perpendicular to AC. So MP is parallel to line from M to midpoint of AC? Actually, both MP and the line from M to midpoint of AC are perpendicular to AC, so they are parallel. But they both pass through M? No, MP passes through M and P; the line from M to midpoint of AC also passes through M. So they are the same line! Because there is a unique line through M perpendicular to AC. Thus the perpendicular bisector of AC is the line through M and the midpoint of AC, which is the same as the line through M perpendicular to AC. So indeed MP is that line. So P lies on the line through M perpendicular to AC. That's consistent.

Thus v = P-A is some vector. We might find that v is proportional to (R^2 - r^2, something). But maybe we can avoid explicit coordinates by using properties of orthocenters and radical axes.

Let me think synthetically.
